!=
0)
{








apply_ad_config(google_ads.length);









render_ads(true,
window['ad_config_top']);









if(window['backfillParam'])
{












window['backfillParam']['pl_rnum']
=
google_ads.length;












window['backfillParam']['pl_time']
=
Date.now()
-
afc_ad_start_time;








}




}
}

function
apply_ad_config(num)
{
window['ad_config_top']
=
Math.min(2,
num);
window['ad_config_bot']
=
Math.min(2,
num);
}

google_ad_client
=
'ca-aj-cat';
google_ad_channel
=
'cx_ask_wiki,ad_dirN';
google_ad_output
=
'js';
google_max_num_ads
=
'4';
google_ad_type
=
'text';
google_image_size
=
'';
google_num_ads_recieved=
'';
google_language
=
'';
google_country
=
'';
google_encoding
=
'utf8';
google_safe
=
'medium';
google_adtest
=
'off';
google_ad_section
=
'default';
google_page_url
=
'';
google_hints
=
'George_Jones';
google_kw
=
'';
google_kw_type
=
'broad';


var
backfillParam
=
{




pl_rqnum
:
google_max_num_ads,




pl_hints
:
google_hints,




pl_rp
:
"false",




pl_kw
:
google_kw,




pl_cl
:
google_ad_client,




pl_ch
:
google_ad_channel,




pl_hex
:
"#FDFEEE",




pl_type
:
"afc",




pl_clksp
:
"none"};

var
word_arr
;

var
word_arr_len
;


function
highlightQuery(input_str)
{

var
queryTokenArray
=
"george,jones,_,";


var
tok_arr
=
queryTokenArray.split(",");

for(tok_arr_idx=0;
tok_arr_idx
<
tok_arr.length-1;
tok_arr_idx
++)
{

if(isCombinationWord(tok_arr[tok_arr_idx])){
word_arr
=
tok_arr[tok_arr_idx].split("-");
word_arr_len
=
word_arr.length;
first_pos
=
input_str.toLowerCase().indexOf(word_arr[0],0);

if(first_pos
!=-1)
{
input_str
=
highlightCombinationWords(input_str,first_pos);
}
}
else
{
first_pos
=
input_str.toLowerCase().indexOf(tok_arr[tok_arr_idx],0);
if(first_pos
!=
-1
&&
tok_arr[tok_arr_idx].length
>
0)
{
input_str
=
highlightSimpleWords(input_str,tok_arr[tok_arr_idx],first_pos);
}
}
}
return
input_str;
}

function
isCombinationWord(word){
if(word.indexOf("-")
!=
-1){
return
true;
}
else
{
return
false;
}
}


function
isAlpha(ch)
{
if
(ch
!=
null
&&
ch.match(/^[a-zA-Z0-9]+$/)){
return
true;
}
return
false;
}

function
isQueryinInput(firstindex,
input_str,
j){
for(i=firstindex;
i<
input_str.length
;
++i)
{
var
currindex
=
i;

if(!isAlpha(input_str.charAt(i)))
{
if(firstindex
==
currindex)

{
firstindex++;
}
else
{
if(word_arr[j]
!=
input_str.substring(firstindex,currindex).toLowerCase())
return
-1;
if
(j
==
word_arr_len-1)
return
currindex;
else
return
isQueryinInput(currindex+1,input_str,++j);
}
}
}

if(j
<
word_arr_len)
{
if(word_arr[j]
!=
input_str.substring(firstindex,currindex+1).toLowerCase())
return
-1;
}

return
(currindex
+
1)
;
}


function

highlightCombinationWords(input_str,first_pos)
{
exactmatch
=
isQueryinInput(first_pos+word_arr[0].length,input_str,1)
;
if(exactmatch
!=-1
)

{
bold_str
=
"<b>"
+
input_str.substring(first_pos,exactmatch)
+
"</b>";
input_str
=
input_str.substring(0,first_pos)
+
bold_str
+
input_str.substring(exactmatch);
next_pos
=
input_str.toLowerCase().indexOf(word_arr[0],exactmatch+7);
while(next_pos
!=
-1)
{
return
highlightCombinationWords(input_str,next_pos);
}
}
return
input_str;
}


function

highlightSimpleWords(input_str,token,
first_pos)
{
var
end_pos
=
first_pos
+
token.length;
if(!isAlpha(input_str.charAt(end_pos))
&&
!isAlpha(input_str.charAt(first_pos-1))){
bold_str
=
"<b>"
+
input_str.substring(first_pos,end_pos)
+
"</b>";
input_str
=
input_str.substring(0,first_pos)
+
bold_str
+
input_str.substring(end_pos);
next_pos
=
input_str.toLowerCase().indexOf(token,end_pos+7);
}
else
{
next_pos
=
input_str.toLowerCase().indexOf(token,end_pos);
}

while(next_pos
!=
-1)
{
return
highlightSimpleWords(input_str,token,next_pos);
}
return
input_str;
}</script>

<p><b>George
Glenn
Jones</b>
(September
12,
1931
–
April
26,
2013)
was
an
American
musician,
singer
and
songwriter
who
achieved
international
fame
for
his
long
list
of
hit
records,
including
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/White_Lightning_(George_Jones_song)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="White
Lightning
(George
Jones
song)">White
Lightning</a>&quot;,
as
well
as
his
distinctive
voice
and
phrasing.
For
the
last
20
years
of
his
life,
Jones
was
frequently
referred
to
as
the
greatest
living
country
singer.<sup
id="cite_ref-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-Allmusic_Biography_2-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Allmusic_Biography-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup>
Country
music
scholar
<a
href="/wiki/Bill_Malone?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Bill
Malone"
class="mw-redirect">Bill
C.
Malone</a>
writes,
&quot;For
the
two
or
three
minutes
consumed
by
a
song,
Jones
immerses
himself
so
completely
in
its
<a
href="/wiki/Lyrics?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Lyrics">lyrics</a>,
and
in
the
mood
it
conveys,
that
the
listener
can
scarcely
avoid
becoming
similarly
involved.&quot;
<a
href="/wiki/Waylon_Jennings?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Waylon
Jennings">Waylon
Jennings</a>
expressed
a
common
jealousy
in
his
song
&quot;It's
Alright&quot;:
&quot;If
we
all
could
sound
like
we
wanted
to,
we'd
all
sound
like
George
Jones.&quot;
The
shape
of
his
nose
and
facial
features
gave
Jones
the
nickname
&quot;The
Possum.&quot;
Jones
said
in
an
interview
that
he
chose
to
tour
only
about
60
dates
a
year.</p>








<p>Born
in
Texas,
Jones
first
heard
country
music
when
he
was
seven
and
was
given
a
guitar
at
the
age
of
nine.
He
married
his
first
wife
Dorothy
Bonvillion
in
1950,
but
ended
in
divorce
in
1951.
He
served
in
the
<a
href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="United
States
Marine
Corps">United
States
Marine
Corps</a>
until
his
discharge
in
1953.
He
later
married
Shirley
Ann
Corley
in
1954.
In
1959,
Jones
released
a
cover
version
of
&quot;White
Lightning&quot;
by
<a
href="/wiki/J._P._Richardson?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="J.
P.
Richardson"
class="mw-redirect">J.
P.
Richardson</a>,
which
launched
his
career
as
a
singer.
After
his
second
marriage
ended
in
divorce
in
1968,
he
married
fellow
country
music
singer
<a
href="/wiki/Tammy_Wynette?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Tammy
Wynette">Tammy
Wynette</a>
a
year
later.
Several
years
of
alcoholism
severely
deteriorated
his
health
and
led
to
Jones
missing
many
performances,
earning
him
the
nickname
&quot;No
Show
Jones.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-Nicknames_3-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Nicknames-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup>
After
his
divorce
from
Wynette
in
1975,
Jones
married
his
fourth
wife,
Nancy
Sepulvado,
in
1983
and
Jones
became
mostly
sober.
Jones
died
in
2013,
aged
81,
from
hypoxic
respiratory
failure.
He
was
buried
in
<a
href="/wiki/Woodlawn_Cemetery?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Woodlawn
Cemetery">Woodlawn
Cemetery</a>.
During
his
life,
Jones
had
more
than
150
hits
during
his
career,
both
as
a
solo
artist
and
in
duets
with
other
artists.</p>








<div
style="overflow:hidden;height:;">

<h2><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Life_and_career">Life
and
career</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=George_Jones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1"
title="Edit
section:
Life
and
career">edit</a>]</span></h2>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Early_years_.281931.E2.80.9353.29">Early
years
(1931–53)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=George_Jones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2"
title="Edit
section:
Early
years
(1931–53)">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<p>George
Glenn
Jones
was
born
on
September
12,
1931
in
<a
href="/wiki/Saratoga,_Texas?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Saratoga,
Texas">Saratoga,
Texas</a>,
and
was
raised
in
<a
href="/wiki/Vidor,_Texas?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Vidor,
Texas">Vidor,
Texas</a>,
with
his
brother
and
five
sisters.<sup
id="cite_ref-isbn0-440-22373-3_4-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-isbn0-440-22373-3-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup>
His
father,
George
Washington
Jones,
worked
in
a
shipyard
and
played
harmonica
and
guitar
while
his
mother,
Clara,
played
piano
in
the
Pentecostal
Church
on
Sundays.<sup
id="cite_ref-CMTNews_5-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-CMTNews-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
During
his
delivery,
one
of
the
doctors
dropped
Jones
and
broke
his
arm.<sup
id="cite_ref-CMTNews_5-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-CMTNews-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
When
he
was
seven,
his
parents
bought
a
radio
and
he
heard
country
music
for
the
first
time.
Jones
recalled
to
<i>Billboard</i>
in
2006
that
he
would
lie
in
bed
with
his
parents
on
Saturday
nights
listening
to
the
<i>Grand
Ole
Opry</i>
and
insist
that
his
mother
wake
him
if
he
fell
asleep
so
he
could
hear
<a
href="/wiki/Roy_Acuff?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Roy
Acuff">Roy
Acuff</a>
or
<a
href="/wiki/Bill_Monroe?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Bill
Monroe">Bill
Monroe</a>.
In
his
autobiography
<i>I
Lived
To
Tell
It
All</i>,
Jones
explains
that
the
early
death
of
his
sister
Ethel
spurred
on
his
father's
drinking
problem
and,
by
all
accounts,
George
Washington
Jones
could
be
physically
and
emotionally
abusive
to
his
wife
and
children
when
he
drank.
In
the
book
<i>George
Jones:
The
Life
and
Times
of
a
Honky
Tonk
Legend</i>,
Bob
Allen
recounts
how
George
Sr.
would
return
home
in
the
middle
of
the
night
with
his
cronies
roaring
drunk,
wake
up
a
terrified
George
Jr.,
and
demand
that
he
sing
for
them
or
face
a
beating.
In
a
CMT
program
on
Jones'
life,
country
music
historian
Robert
K.
Oermann
marveled,
&quot;You
would
think
that
it
would
make
him
not
a
singer,
because
it
was
so
abusively
thrust
on
him.
But
the
opposite
happened;
he
became
a
chronic
singer.
He
became
someone
who
had
to
sing.&quot;
In
the
same
program,
Jones
admitted
that
he
remained
ambivalent
and
resentful
towards
his
father
up
until
the
day
he
died
and
observed
in
his
autobiography
&quot;The
Jones
family
makeup
doesn't
sit
well
with
liquor...Daddy
was
an
unusual
drinker.
He
drank
to
excess
but
never
while
working,
and
he
probably
was
the
hardest
working
man
I've
ever
known.&quot;
His
father
bought
him
his
first
guitar
at
age
nine
and
he
learned
his
first
chords
and
songs
at
church
and
there
are
several
photographs
of
a
young
George
busking
on
the
streets
of
Beaumont.</p>








<p>He
left
home
at
16
and
went
to
<a
href="/wiki/Jasper,_Texas?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Jasper,
Texas">Jasper,
Texas</a>,
where
he
sang
and
played
on
the
KTXJ
radio
station
with
fellow
musician
Dalton
Henderson.
From
there,
he
worked
at
the
KRIC
radio
station.
During
one
such
afternoon
show,
Jones
met
his
idol,
<a
href="/wiki/Hank_Williams?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Hank
Williams">Hank
Williams</a>
(&quot;I
just
stared,&quot;
he
later
wrote).<sup
id="cite_ref-CMTNews_5-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-CMTNews-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
In
the
1989
video
documentary
<i>Same
Ole
Me</i>,
Jones
admitted,
&quot;I
couldn't
think
or
eat
nothin'
unless
it
was
Hank
Williams,
and
I
couldn't
wait
for
his
next
record
to
come
out.
He
had
to
be,
really,
the
greatest.&quot;
He
married
his
first
wife
Dorothy
Bonvillion
in
1950,
but
they
divorced
in
1951.
He
was
enlisted
in
the
<a
href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="United
States
Marine
Corps">United
States
Marine
Corps</a>
until
his
discharge
in
1953.
He
was
stationed
in
San
Jose,
<a
href="/wiki/California?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="California">California</a>
for
his
entire
service.<sup
id="cite_ref-isbn0-87930-760-9_6-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-isbn0-87930-760-9-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="First_recordings_.281954.E2.80.9357.29">First
recordings
(1954–57)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=George_Jones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3"
title="Edit
section:
First
recordings
(1954–57)">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<p>Jones
married
Shirley
Ann
Corley
in
1954.
His
first
record,
the
self-penned
&quot;No
Money
in
This
Deal&quot;,
appeared
in
February
1954
on
Starday
Records
and
began
the
singer's
association
with
producer
and
mentor
H.W.
&quot;Pappy&quot;
Dailey.
The
song
was
actually
cut
in
Jack
Starnes'
living
room
(Starnes
formed
Starday
Records
with
Daily).
Jones
also
worked
at
KRTM
in
Beaumont
around
this
time.
Deejay
Gordon
Baxter
told
Nick
Tosches
that
Jones
acquired
the
nickname
&quot;possum&quot;
while
working
there:
&quot;One
of
the
deejays
there,
Slim
Watts,
took
to
calling
him
George
P.
Willicker
Picklepuss
Possum
Jones.
For
one
thing,
he
cut
his
hair
short,
like
a
possum's
belly.
He
had
a
possum's
nose
and
stupid
eyes,
like
a
possum.&quot;
During
his
early
recording
sessions,
Daily
admonished
Jones
for
attempting
to
sound
too
much
like
his
heroes
Hank
Williams
and
<a
href="/wiki/Lefty_Frizzell?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Lefty
Frizzell">Lefty
Frizzell</a>.
Jones'
first
hit
came
with
&quot;Why
Baby
Why&quot;
in
1955.
That
same
year,
while
touring
as
a
cast
member
of
the
<i>Louisiana
Hayride</i>,
Jones
met
and
played
shows
with
<a
href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Elvis
Presley">Elvis
Presley</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Johnny_Cash?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Johnny
Cash">Johnny
Cash</a>.
&quot;I
didn't
get
to
know
him
that
well,&quot;
Jones
said
of
Presley
to
Nick
Tosches
in
1994.
&quot;He
stayed
pretty
much
with
his
friends
around
him
in
his
dressing
room.
Nobody
seemed
to
get
around
him
much
any
length
of
time
to
talk
to
him.&quot;
Jones
would,
however,
remain
a
lifelong
friend
of
Johnny
Cash.
Jones
was
invited
to
sing
at
the
<i>Grand
Ole
Opry</i>
in
1956.</p>








<p>With
Presley's
explosion
in
popularity
in
1956,
pressure
was
put
on
Jones
to
cut
a
few
rockabilly
sides
and
he
reluctantly
agreed.
His
heart
was
never
in
it,
however,
and
he
quickly
regretted
the
decision;
in
his
autobiography
he
joked,
&quot;During
the
years,
when
I've
encountered
those
records,
I've
used
them
for
Frisbees.&quot;
He
explained
to
<i>Billboard</i>
in
2006:
&quot;I
was
desperate.
When
you're
hungry,
a
poor
man
with
a
house
full
of
kids,
you're
gonna
do
some
things
you
ordinarily
wouldn't
do.
I
said,
'Well,
hell,
I'll
try
anything
once.'
I
tried
&quot;Dadgum
It
How
Come
It&quot;
and
&quot;Rock
It&quot;,
a
bunch
of
shit.
I
didn't
want
my
name
on
the
rock
and
roll
thing,
so
I
told
them
to
put
Thumper
Jones
on
it
and
if
it
did
something,
good,
if
it
didn't,
hell,
I
didn't
want
to
be
shamed
with
it.&quot;
Jones
went
on
to
say
he
unsuccessfully
attempted
to
buy
all
the
masters
to
keep
the
cuts
from
surfacing
later,
which
they
did.<sup
id="cite_ref-7"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>Jones
moved
to
Mercury
in
1957.
In
early
1957
Jones
teamed
up
with
singer
Jeannette
Hicks,
the
first
of
several
duet
partners
he
would
have
over
the
years,
and
enjoyed
yet
another
Top
Ten
single
with
&quot;Yearning.&quot;
Starday
Records
merged
with
Mercury
that
same
year,
and
Jones
scored
high
marks
on
the
charts
with
his
debut
Mercury
release
of
&quot;Don't
Stop
the
Music.&quot;
Meanwhile,
George
was
travelling
the
black-top
roads
in
a
1940s
Packard
with
his
name
and
phone
number
emblazoned
on
the
side.
Although
he
was
garnering
a
lot
of
attention
and
his
singles
were
making
very
respectable
showings
on
the
charts,
Jones
was
still
playing
the
&quot;blood
bucket&quot;
circuit
of
honky-tonks
that
dotted
the
rural
countryside.<sup
id="cite_ref-8"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Commercial_breakout_.281959.E2.80.9364.29">Commercial
breakout
(1959–64)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=George_Jones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4"
title="Edit
section:
Commercial
breakout
(1959–64)">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<p>In
1959,
he
had
his
first
number
one
on
the
Billboard
country
chart
with
&quot;White
Lightnin'&quot;,
ironically
a
more
authentic
rock
and
roll
sound
than
his
half-hearted
rockabilly
cuts.
In
the
<i>Same
Ole
Me</i>
retrospective,
Johnny
Cash
insisted,
&quot;George
Jones
woulda
been
a
really
hot
rockabilly
artist
if
he'd
approached
it
from
that
angle.
Well,
<i>he
was</i>,
really,
but
never
got
the
credit
for
it.&quot;
&quot;White
Lightnin'&quot;
was
written
by
<a
href="/wiki/J.P._Richardson?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="J.P.
Richardson"
class="mw-redirect">J.P.
Richardson</a>,
better
known
as
the
Big
Bopper.
In
<i>I
Lived
To
Tell
It
All</i>,
Jones
confessed
that
he
showed
up
for
the
recording
session
under
the
influence
of
a
great
deal
of
alcohol
and
it
took
him
approximately
80
takes
just
to
record
his
vocals.
To
make
matters
worse,
Buddy
Killen,
who
played
the
upright
bass
on
the
recording,
was
reported
as
having
severely
blistered
fingers
from
having
to
play
his
bass
part
80
times.
Killen
not
only
threatened
to
quit
the
session,
but
also
threatened
to
physically
harm
Jones
for
the
painful
consequences
of
Jones'
drinking.
On
the
final
vocal
take
used
on
the
recording
Jones
slurs
the
word
&quot;slug&quot;,
something
he
would
mimic
in
live
performances
of
the
song.</p>








<p>One
aspect
of
Jones'
early
career
that
is
often
overlooked
is
his
success
as
a
songwriter;
he
wrote
or
co-wrote
many
of
his
biggest
hits
during
this
period,
several
of
which
have
become
standards,
like
&quot;The
Window
Up
Above&quot;
(later
a
smash
for
<a
href="/wiki/Mickey_Gilley?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Mickey
Gilley">Mickey
Gilley</a>
in
1975)
and
&quot;Seasons
Of
My
Heart&quot;
(a
hit
for
Johnny
Cash
and
also
recorded
by
<a
href="/wiki/Willie_Nelson?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Willie
Nelson">Willie
Nelson</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Jerry_Lee_Lewis?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Jerry
Lee
Lewis">Jerry
Lee
Lewis</a>).
Jones
wrote
&quot;Just
One
More&quot;
(also
recorded
by
Cash),
&quot;Life
To
Go&quot;
(a
top
five
hit
for
<a
href="/wiki/Stonewall_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Stonewall
Jackson">Stonewall
Jackson</a>
in
1959),
&quot;You
Gotta
Be
My
Baby&quot;
and
&quot;Don't
Stop
The
Music&quot;
on
his
own
and
had
a
hand
in
writing
&quot;Color
Of
The
Blues&quot;
(covered
by
<a
href="/wiki/Elvis_Costello?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Elvis
Costello">Elvis
Costello</a>),
&quot;Tender
Years&quot;
and
&quot;Tall
Tall
Trees&quot;
(co-written
with
<a
href="/wiki/Roger_Miller?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Roger
Miller">Roger
Miller</a>).
Jones'
most
frequent
songwriting
collaborator
was
his
childhood
friend
Darrell
Edwards.</p>








<p>Jones
signed
with
United
Artists
in
1962
and
immediately
scored
one
of
the
biggest
hits
of
his
career,
&quot;She
Thinks
I
Still
Care&quot;.
His
voice
had
grown
noticeably
deeper
during
this
period
and
he
began
cultivating
the
singing
style
that
became
uniquely
his
own.
During
his
stint
with
UA,
Jones
recorded
tribute
albums
to
Hank
Williams
and
<a
href="/wiki/Bob_Wills?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Bob
Wills">Bob
Wills</a>
and
cut
an
album
of
duets
with
<a
href="/wiki/Melba_Montgomery?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Melba
Montgomery">Melba
Montgomery</a>,
including
the
hit
&quot;We
Must
Have
Been
Out
Of
Our
Minds&quot;.
Jones
was
also
well
on
his
way
to
gaining
a
reputation
as
a
notorious
hell-raiser.
In
his
<i>Rolling
Stone</i>
tribute
Merle
Haggard
recalls,
&quot;I
met
him
at
the
Blackboard
Caf&eacute;
in
Bakersfield,
California,
which
was
the
place
to
go
in
'61.
He
was
already
famous
for
not
showing
up
or
showing
up
drunk,
and
he
showed
up
drunk.
I
was
onstage
-
I
think
I
was
singing
Marty
Robbins'
&quot;Devil
Woman&quot;
-
and
he
kicked
the
doors
of
the
office
open
and
said
'Who
the
fuck
is
that?'
It
was
one
the
greatest
compliments
of
my
entire
life
when
George
Jones
said
I
was
his
favorite
country
singer...In
1967,
I
released
a
ballad
called
&quot;I
Threw
Away
The
Rose&quot;
and
he
was
so
impressed
he
actually
jumped
ship
and
left
his
tour,
rented
a
Lear
Jet
and
came
to
Amarillo,
Texas.
He
told
me
my
low
note
changed
his
life.
He
also
folded
my
steel
guitarist
Fuzzy
Owen
in
a
rollaway
bed
and
rolled
him
out
on
the
street.
That
was
the
pinnacle.&quot;
Former
president
of
Starday
Records
Don
Pierce
told
director
Mark
Hall
in
1989
another
famous
story
about
Jones
after
Pappy
Daily
bailed
him
out
of
the
drunk
tank
and
got
him
a
gig
in
Houston
for
$2,500.
The
next
day
Jones
came
to
Dailey's
office
broke
again.
According
to
Pierce,
an
irritated
Dailey
said,
&quot;Well,
George,
you
just
made
$2,500
but
I
talked
to
some
of
the
guys
you
were
out
partying
with
and
they
said
you
went
and
flushed
it
down
the
toilet.&quot;
&quot;Pappy,
that's
a
damn
lie!&quot;
Jones
shot
back.
&quot;It
wasn't
but
$1,200.&quot;</p>








<p>On
tour
Jones
was
always
backed
by
the
Jones
Boys.
Like
Buck
Owens'
Buckaroos
and
Merle
Haggard's
Strangers,
Jones
worked
with
many
musicians
who
were
great
talents
in
their
own
right.
These
included
<a
href="/wiki/Daniel_Joseph_Schafer?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Daniel
Joseph
Schafer">Dan
Schafer</a>,<sup
id="cite_ref-9"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup>
Hank
Singer,
Brittany
Allyn,
Sonny
Curtis,
Kent
Goodson,
Bobby
Birkhead,
and
Steve
Hinson.
In
the
1980s
and
1990s,
bass
player
Ron
Gaddis
served
as
the
Jones
Boys'
bandleader
and
sang
harmony
with
George
in
concert.
<a
href="/wiki/Lorrie_Morgan?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Lorrie
Morgan">Lorrie
Morgan</a>
(who
was
once
married
to
Gaddis)
also
toured
as
a
backup
singer
for
Jones
in
the
late
1970s
and
early
1980s.
<a
href="/wiki/Johnny_Paycheck?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Johnny
Paycheck">Johnny
Paycheck</a>
was
the
Jones
Boys'
bass
player
in
the
1960s
before
going
on
to
his
own
stardom
in
the
1970s.</p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Alcoholism_and_decline_.281964.E2.80.9379.29">Alcoholism
and
decline
(1964–79)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=George_Jones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5"
title="Edit
section:
Alcoholism
and
decline
(1964–79)">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<p>In
1964,
Pappy
Daily
secured
a
new
contract
with
Musicor
records.
For
the
rest
of
the
1960s,
Jones
would
only
score
one
number
one
(1967's
&quot;Walk
Through
This
World
With
Me&quot;)
but
he
practically
owned
the
country
music
charts
throughout
the
decade.
Significant
hits
include
&quot;Love
Bug&quot;
(a
nod
to
<a
href="/wiki/Buck_Owens?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Buck
Owens">Buck
Owens</a>
and
the
Bakersfield
sound),
&quot;Things
Have
Gone
To
Pieces&quot;,
&quot;The
Race
Is
On&quot;,
&quot;My
Favorite
Lies&quot;,
&quot;I'll
Share
My
World
With
You&quot;,
&quot;Take
Me&quot;
(a
song
he
co-wrote
and
would
later
record
with
Tammy
Wynette),
&quot;A
Good
Year
For
The
Roses&quot;
and
&quot;If
My
Heart
Had
Windows&quot;.
By
this
point,
Jones'
singing
style
had
evolved
from
the
full-throated,
high
lonesome
sound
of
Hank
Williams
and
Roy
Acuff
on
his
early
Starday
records
to
the
more
refined,
subtle
style
of
Lefty
Frizzell.
In
a
2006
interview
with
<i>Billboard</i>,
Jones
acknowledged
the
Texan's
influence
on
his
idiosyncratic
phrasing:
&quot;I
got
that
from
Lefty.
He
always
made
five
syllables
out
of
one
damn
word.&quot;</p>








<p>Jones'
binge
drinking
and
use
of
amphetamines
on
the
road
caught
up
to
him
in
1967
and
he
had
to
be
admitted
into
a
neurological
hospital
to
seek
treatment
for
his
drinking.
Jones
would
go
to
extreme
lengths
for
a
drink
if
the
thirst
was
on
him.
Perhaps
the
most
famous
drinking
story
concerning
Jones
occurred
while
he
was
married
to
his
second
wife
Shirley
Corley.
Jones
recalled
Shirley
making
it
physically
impossible
for
him
to
travel
to
<a
href="/wiki/Beaumont,_Texas?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Beaumont,
Texas">Beaumont</a>,
located
8
miles
away,
to
buy
liquor.
Because
Jones
would
not
walk
that
far,
she
would
hide
the
keys
to
each
of
their
cars
they
owned
before
leaving.
She
did
not,
however,
hide
the
keys
to
the
lawn
mower.
Upset,
Jones
walked
to
the
window
and
looked
out
over
his
property.
He
later
described
his
thoughts
in
his
memoir:
&quot;There,
gleaming
in
the
glow,
was
that
ten-horsepower
rotary
engine
under
a
seat.
A
key
glistening
in
the
ignition.
I
imagine
the
top
speed
for
that
old
mower
was
five
miles
per
hour.
It
might
have
taken
an
hour
and
a
half
or
more
for
me
to
get
to
the
liquor
store,
but
get
there
I
did.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-10"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup><sup
class="noprint
Inline-Template"
style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a
href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources"
target="_blank"
title="Wikipedia:Citing
sources"><span
title="This
citation
requires
a
reference
to
the
specific
page
or
range
of
pages
in
which
the
material
appears.
(May
2013)">page&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
Years
later
Jones
comically
mocked
the
incident
by
making
a
cameo
in
the
video
for
&quot;All
My
Rowdy
Friends
Are
Coming
Over
Tonight&quot;
by
<a
href="/wiki/Hank_Williams,_Jr.?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Hank
Williams,
Jr.">Hank
Williams,
Jr.</a>.
He
also
parodied
the
episode
in
the
1993
video
for
&quot;One
More
Last
Chance&quot;
by
<a
href="/wiki/Vince_Gill?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Vince
Gill">Vince
Gill</a>
and
in
his
own
music
video
for
the
single
&quot;Honky
Tonk
Song&quot;
in
1996.
Curiously,
in
her
1979
autobiography
<i>Stand
By
Your
Man</i>,
Tammy
Wynette
claims
the
incident
occurred
while
she
was
married
to
Jones,
maintaining
that
she
woke
up
at
one
o'clock
in
the
morning
to
find
her
husband
gone:
&quot;I
got
into
the
car
and
drove
to
the
nearest
bar
10
miles
away.
When
I
pulled
into
the
parking
lot
there
sat
our
rider-mower
right
by
the
entrance.
He'd
driven
that
mower
right
down
a
main
highway.
He
looked
up
and
saw
me
and
said,
‘Well,
fellas,
here
she
is
now.
My
little
wife,
I
told
you
she'd
come
after
me.’&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-11"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup
class="noprint
Inline-Template"
style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a
href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources"
target="_blank"
title="Wikipedia:Citing
sources"><span
title="This
citation
requires
a
reference
to
the
specific
page
or
range
of
pages
in
which
the
material
appears.
(May
2013)">page&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>








<p>Jones
became
aware
of
Tammy
Wynette
because
their
tours
were
booked
by
the
same
agency
and
their
paths
sometimes
crossed
after
Wynette's
first
minor
hit
&quot;Apartment
#9&quot;
in
1966.
Wynette
was
married
to
songwriter
Don
Chapel,
who
was
also
the
opening
act
for
her
shows
at
the
time.
The
three
became
friends
but
eventually
Jones
took
more
than
a
passing
fancy
to
Wynette,
who
was
eleven
years
his
junior
and
grew
up
listening
to
all
of
his
records.
According
to
his
autobiography,
Jones
went
to
their
house
for
supper
and
while
she
was
fixing
the
meal
Wynette
and
Chapel
got
into
a
heated
exchange
with
Chapel
calling
his
wife
&quot;a
son
of
a
bitch&quot;.
Jones
wrote:
&quot;I
felt
rage
fly
all
over
me.
I
jumped
from
my
chair,
put
my
hands
under
the
dinner
table,
and
flipped
it
over.
Dishes,
utensils,
and
glasses
flew
in
all
directions.
Don's
and
Tammy's
eyes
got
about
as
big
as
the
flying
dinner
plates.&quot;
Jones
professed
his
love
for
Wynette
on
the
spot
and
the
couple
were
married
in
1969.</p>








<p>They
began
touring
together
and
Jones
bought
out
his
contract
with
Musicor
so
he
could
record
with
Tammy
and
her
producer
<a
href="/wiki/Billy_Sherrill?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Billy
Sherrill">Billy
Sherrill</a>
on
<a
href="/wiki/Epic_Records?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Epic
Records">Epic
Records</a>
(the
singer
had
split
with
longtime
producer
Pappy
Daily
on
acrimonious
terms).
Jones
and
Wynette
became
known
as
&quot;Mr.
&amp;
Mrs.
Country
Music&quot;
in
the
early
1970s,
scoring
several
big
hits,
including
&quot;We're
Gonna
Hold
On,&quot;
&quot;Let's
Build
A
World
Together&quot;,
&quot;Golden
Ring&quot;,
&quot;Near
You&quot;
and
&quot;(We're
Not)
The
Jet
Set&quot;.
In
October
1970,
shortly
after
the
birth
of
their
only
child
Tamala
Georgette,
Jones
was
straightjacketed
and
committed
to
a
padded
cell
at
the
Watson
Clinic
in
Lakeland,
Florida
after
a
drunken
bender;
he
was
kept
there
to
detoxify
for
ten
days
before
being
released
with
a
prescription
for
Librium.
Jones
managed
longer
stretches
of
sobriety
with
Wynette
than
he
had
enjoyed
in
years
but
as
the
decade
wore
on
his
drinking
and
erratic
behavior
worsened,
leading
to
the
couple's
divorce
in
1976.
Jones
accepted
the
responsibility
for
the
failure
of
the
marriage
but
vehemently
denied
Wynette's
allegations
in
her
autobiography
that
he
beat
her
and
fired
a
shotgun
at
her.</p>








<p>Remarkably,
Jones
and
Wynette
continued
playing
shows
and
drawing
crowds
in
the
years
after
their
divorce,
as
fans
began
to
see
their
songs
mirroring
their
stormy
relationship.
In
1980,
they
recorded
the
album
<i>Together
Again</i>
and
scored
a
hit
with
&quot;Two
Story
House&quot;.
Jones
also
spoke
publicly
about
his
hopes
for
a
reconciliation
and
would
jokingly
reference
Tammy
in
some
of
his
songs
-
during
performances
of
his
1981
hit
&quot;If
Drinkin'
Don't
Kill
Me
(Her
Memory
Will)&quot;
he
would
sing
&quot;Tammy's
memory
will&quot;
-
but
the
recrimination
continued
unabated.
After
years
of
sniping,
Jones
and
Wynette
appeared
to
make
peace
in
the
1990s,
recording
a
final
album,
<i>One</i>,
and
even
touring
together
again
before
Wynette's
death
in
1998.
In
1995
Jones
told
<i>Country
Weekly</i>,
&quot;Like
the
old
saying
goes,
it
takes
time
to
heal
things
and
they've
been
healed
quite
a
while.&quot;</p>








<p>Jones
pairing
with
Billy
Sherrill
at
Epic
Records
came
as
a
surprise
to
many;
Sherrill
and
business
partner
Glenn
Sutton
are
regarded
as
the
defining
influences
of
the
countrypolitan
sound,
a
smooth
amalgamation
of
pop
and
country
music
that
was
popular
during
the
late
1960s
and
throughout
the
1970s,
a
far
cry
from
what
was
George's
honky
tonk
roots.
Despite
a
shaky
start,
the
success
that
Sherrill
had
with
Jones
proved
to
be
his
most
enduring;
although
<i>Billboard</i>
chart
statistics
show
that
Sherrill
had
his
biggest
commercial
successes
with
artists
such
as
Wynette
and
<a
href="/wiki/Charlie_Rich?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Charlie
Rich">Charlie
Rich</a>,
with
Jones,
Sherrill
had
his
most
longest-lasting
association.
In
1974,
they
scored
a
number
one
hit
with
&quot;The
Door&quot;
(<i>I've
heard
the
sound
of
my
dear
old
mother
cryin'/and
the
sound
of
the
train
that
took
me
off
to
war</i>)
and
followed
that
in
1975
with
&quot;The
Grand
Tour&quot;.
Unlike
most
singers,
who
might
have
been
overwhelmed
by
the
string
arrangements
and
background
vocalists
Sherrill
sometimes
employed
on
his
records,
Jones'
voice,
with
its
at
times
frightening
intensity
and
lucid
tone,
could
stand
up
to
anything.
While
Jones
wrote
fewer
songs
himself
-
songwriters
had
been
tripping
over
themselves
pitching
songs
to
him
for
years
-
he
still
managed
to
co-write
several,
such
as
&quot;What
My
Woman
Can't
Do&quot;
(covered
by
Jerry
Lee
Lewis),
&quot;A
Drunk
Can't
Be
A
Man&quot;,
the
harrowing
&quot;I
Just
Don't
Give
A
Damn&quot;
(perhaps
the
greatest
&quot;lost
classic&quot;
in
the
entire
Jones
catalogue)
and
&quot;These
Days
(I
Barely
Get
By)&quot;,
which
he
had
written
with
Wynette.</p>








<p>In
the
late
seventies,
Jones
spiraled
out
of
control.
Already
drinking
constantly,
a
manager
named
Shug
Baggot
introduced
him
to
<a
href="/wiki/Cocaine?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Cocaine">cocaine</a>
before
a
show
because
he
was
too
tired
to
perform.
The
drug
would
increase
Jones'
already
considerable
paranoia.
During
one
drunken
binge
he
shot
at,
and
very
nearly
hit,
his
friend
and
occasional
songwriting
partner
Earl
&quot;Peanutt&quot;
Montgomery
after
Montgomery
had
quit
drinking
after
finding
religion.
He
was
often
penniless
and
acknowledged
in
his
autobiography
that
<a
href="/wiki/Waylon_Jennings?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Waylon
Jennings">Waylon
Jennings</a>
and
Johnny
Cash
came
to
his
financial
aid
during
this
time.
Jones
also
began
missing
shows
at
an
alarming
rate
and
lawsuits
from
promoters
started
piling
up.
In
1978,
owing
Wynette
$36,000
in
child
support
and
claiming
to
be
one
million
dollars
in
debt,
he
filed
for
bankruptcy.
Jones
appeared
incoherent
at
times,
speaking
in
quarrelling
voices
that
he
would
later
call
&quot;the
Duck&quot;
and
&quot;the
Old
Man&quot;.
In
his
article
&quot;The
Devil
In
George
Jones&quot;,
Nick
Tosches
states,
&quot;By
February
1979
he
was
homeless,
deranged,
and
destitute,
living
in
his
car
and
barely
able
to
digest
the
junk
food
on
which
he
subsisted.
He
weighed
under
a
hundred
pounds,
and
his
condition
was
so
bad
that
it
took
him
more
than
two
years
to
complete
<i>My
Very
Special
Guests</i>,
an
album
on
which
Willie
Nelson,
<a
href="/wiki/Linda_Ronstadt?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Linda
Ronstadt">Linda
Ronstadt</a>,
Elvis
Costello,
and
other
famous
fans
came
to
his
vocal
aid
and
support.
Jones
entered
Hillcrest
Psychiatric
Hospital
in
Birmingham,
Alabama.
Upon
his
release
in
January
1980,
the
first
thing
he
did
was
pick
up
a
six-pack.&quot;</p>








<p>Jones
often
displayed
a
sheepish,
self-deprecating
sense
of
humor
regarding
his
dire
financial
standing
and
bad
reputation.
In
June
1979,
he
appeared
with
Waylon
Jennings
on
Ralph
Emery's
syndicated
radio
program
and
at
one
point
Jennings
cracked,
&quot;It's
lonely
at
the
top.&quot;
A
laughing
Jones
replied,
&quot;It's
lonely
at
the
bottom,
too!
It's
real,
real
lonely,
Waylon.&quot;
Despite
his
chronic
unreliability,
Jones
was
still
capable
of
putting
on
a
captivating
live
show.
On
Independence
Day,
1976,
he
appeared
at
Willie
Nelson's
Fourth
of
July
Picnic
in
Gonzales,
Texas
in
front
of
80,000
younger,
country
rock
oriented
fans.
A
nervous
Jones
felt
out
of
his
comfort
zone
and
nearly
bolted
from
the
festival
but
went
on
anyway
and
wound
up
stealing
the
show.
<i>The
Houston
Post</i>
wrote,
&quot;He
was
the
undisputed
star
of
this
year's
Willie
Nelson
picnic...one
of
the
greatest.&quot;
<i>Penthouse</i>
called
him
&quot;...the
spirit
of
country
music,
plain
and
simple,
its
Holy
Ghost&quot;.
<i>The
Village
Voice</i>
added
&quot;As
a
singer
he
is
as
intelligent
as
they
come,
and
should
be
considered
for
a
spot
in
America's
all-time
top
ten.&quot;
Jones
began
missing
more
shows
than
he
made,
however,
including
several
highly
publicized
dates
at
the
Bottom
Line
club
in
New
York.
Former
vice
president
of
CBS
Records
Rick
Blackburn
recalls
in
the
1989
video
<i>Same
Ole
Me</i>
that
the
event
had
been
hyped
for
weeks,
with
a
lot
of
top
press
and
cast
members
from
<i>Saturday
Night
Live</i>
planning
to
attend.
&quot;We'd
made
our
plans,
travel
arrangements
and
so
forth.
George
excused
himself
from
my
office,
left
-
and
we
didn't
see
him
for
three
weeks.
He
just
<i>did
not
show
up</i>.&quot;
Much
like
Hank
Williams,
Jones
seemed
suspicious
of
success
and
furiously
despised
perceived
slights
and
condescension
directed
towards
the
music
that
he
loved
so
dearly.
When
he
finally
played
the
Bottom
Line
in
1980,
the
<i>New
York
Times</i>
called
him
&quot;the
finest,
most
riveting
singer
in
country
music.&quot;</p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Comeback_.281980.E2.80.931990.29">Comeback
(1980–1990)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=George_Jones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6"
title="Edit
section:
Comeback
(1980–1990)">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<p>By
1980,
Jones
had
not
had
a
number
one
single
in
six
years
and
many
critics
began
to
write
him
off.
However,
the
singer
stunned
the
music
industry
in
April
when
&quot;He
Stopped
Loving
Her
Today&quot;
was
released
and
shot
to
number
one
on
the
country
charts,
remaining
there
for
18
weeks.
The
song
was
written
by
<a
href="/wiki/Bobby_Braddock?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Bobby
Braddock">Bobby
Braddock</a>
and
Curly
Putnam
and
tells
the
story
of
a
friend
who
has
never
given
up
on
his
love;
he
keeps
old
letters
and
photos
from
back
in
the
day
and
hangs
on
to
hope
that
she
would
&quot;come
back
again&quot;.
The
song
reaches
its
peak
in
the
chorus,
revealing
that
he
indeed
stopped
loving
her
when
he
died.
The
woman
does
return—for
his
funeral.
In
a
lesser
singer's
hands,
the
song
might
have
sounded
corny
or
even
comical
but
Jones'
interpretation,
buoyed
by
his
brilliant
delivery
of
the
line
&quot;...first
time
I'd
seen
him
smile
in
years&quot;,
gives
it
a
mournful,
gripping
realism.
When
it
began
being
played
on
the
radio
in
the
spring
of
1980
just
about
everyone
who
heard
it
was
floored.
It
is
consistently
voted
as
the
greatest
country
song
of
all
time,
along
with
&quot;I'm
So
Lonesome
I
Could
Cry&quot;
by
Hank
Williams
and
&quot;Crazy&quot;
by
<a
href="/wiki/Patsy_Cline?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Patsy
Cline">Patsy
Cline</a>.<sup
class="Template-Fact"
style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a
href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed"
target="_blank"
title="Wikipedia:Citation
needed"><span
title="This
claim
needs
references
to
reliable
sources.
(January
2014)">citation
needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>








<p>According
to
producer
Billy
Sherrill
and
Jones
himself,
the
singer
hated
the
song
when
he
first
heard
it.
In
Bob
Allen's
biography
of
the
singer,
Sherrill
states,
&quot;He
thought
it
was
too
long,
too
sad,
too
depressing
and
that
nobody
would
ever
play
it...He
hated
the
melody
and
wouldn't
learn
it.&quot;
Sherrill
also
claims
that
Jones
frustrated
him
by
continually
singing
the
song
to
the
melody
of
the
<a
href="/wiki/Kris_Kristofferson?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Kris
Kristofferson">Kris
Kristofferson</a>
hit
&quot;Help
Me
Make
It
Through
the
Night&quot;.
In
the
<i>Same
Ole
Me</i>
retrospective,
Sherrill
recalls
a
heated
exchange
during
one
recording
session:
&quot;I
said
'That's
not
the
melody!'
and
he
said
'Yeah,
but
it's
a
better
melody.'
I
said
'It
might
be—Kristofferson
would
think
so
too,
it's
his
melody!'&quot;
In
the
same
documentary,
Sherrill
claims
that
Jones
was
in
such
bad
physical
shape
during
this
period
that
&quot;the
recitation
was
recorded
18
months
after
the
first
verse
was&quot;
and
added
that
the
last
words
Jones
said
about
&quot;He
Stopped
Loving
Her
Today&quot;
was
&quot;Nobody'll
buy
that
morbid
son
of
a
bitch&quot;.
A
big
part
of
Jones'
success
over
the
years
was
that
he
could
always
smell
a
hit
but
this
time
his
instincts
were
woefully
off.
Although
he
had
disliked
&quot;He
Stopped
Loving
Her
Today&quot;
when
it
was
first
offered
to
him,
Jones
ultimately
gave
the
song
credit
for
reviving
his
flagging
career,
stating
that
&quot;a
four-decade
career
had
been
salvaged
by
a
three-minute
song.&quot;
Jones
earned
the
Grammy
Award
for
Best
Male
Country
Vocal
Performance
in
1980.
The
Academy
of
Country
Music
awarded
the
song
Single
of
the
Year
and
Song
of
the
Year
in
1980.
It
also
became
the
Country
Music
Association's
Song
of
the
Year
in
both
1980
and
1981.</p>








<p>The
success
of
&quot;He
Stopped
Loving
Her
Today&quot;
led
CBS
Records
to
renew
Jones'
recording
contract
and
sparked
new
interest
in
the
singer.
He
was
the
subject
of
an
hour-and-a-quarter
long
HBO
television
special
entitled
<i>George
Jones:
With
a
Little
Help
from
His
Friends</i>,
which
saw
the
painfully
wan
singer
performing
songs
with
Waylon
Jennings,
Elvis
Costello,
<a
href="/wiki/Tanya_Tucker?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Tanya
Tucker">Tanya
Tucker</a>,
and
Tammy
Wynette,
among
others.
Jones
continued
drinking
and
using
cocaine,
appearing
at
various
awards
shows
to
accept
honors
for
&quot;He
Stopped
Loving
Her
Today&quot;
obviously
inebriated;
his
condition
was
readily
apparent
when
he
slurred
the
words
to
&quot;I
Was
Country
When
Country
Wasn't
Cool&quot;
with
<a
href="/wiki/Barbara_Mandrell?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Barbara
Mandrell">Barbara
Mandrell</a>
on
a
televised
broadcast.
He
was
involved
in
several
high
speed
car
chases
with
police,
which
were
reported
on
the
national
news,
and
one
arrest
was
filmed
by
a
local
TV
crew;
the
video,
which
is
widely
available
online,
offers
a
glimpse
into
Jones'
Jekyll
&amp;
Hyde
persona
when
drinking,
with
the
singer
looking
genuinely
frightening
as
he
argues
with
the
police
officer
and
lunges
at
the
camera
man.
Conversely,
when
sober,
Jones
was
known
to
be
friendly
and
down
to
earth,
even
shy.
In
a
1994
article
on
Jones,
Nick
Tosches
remarked
that
when
he
first
interviewed
the
singer
in
April
1976,
&quot;One
could
readily
believe
the
accounts
by
those
who
had
known
him
for
years:
that
he
had
not
changed
much
at
all
and
that
he
had
been
impervious
to
fame
and
fortune.&quot;
In
an
unusually
unguarded
self-appraisal
in
1981,
the
singer
told
Mark
Rose
of
<i>The
Village
Voice</i>,
&quot;I
don't
show
a
lot
of
affection.
I
have
probably
been
a
very
unliked
person
among
family,
like
somebody
who
was
heartless.
I
saved
it
all
for
the
songs.
I
didn't
know
you
were
suppose
to
show
that
love
person
to
person.
I
guess
I
always
wanted
to,
but
I
didn't
know
how.
The
only
way
I
could
would
be
to
do
it
in
a
song.&quot;
Years
later
he
commented
to
The
Christian
Broadcasting
Network's
Scott
Ross,
&quot;I
think
you're
mad
at
yourself,
I
think
that
you're
sayin'
to
yourself
'You
don't
deserve
this.
You
don't
deserve
those
fans.
You
don't
deserve
makin'
this
money.'
And
you're
mad
at
yourself.
And
you
beat
up
on
yourself
by
drinkin'
and
losing
friends
that
won't
put
up
with
that...It's
just
one
terrible
big
mess
you
make
out
of
your
life.&quot;
In
1982,
Jones
recorded
the
album
<i>A
Taste
of
Yesterday's
Wine</i>
with
Merle
Haggard.
While
the
album
cover
was
shocking,
with
Jones
looking
gaunt
and
wasted,
his
singing
was
flawless.
His
run
of
hits
also
continued
in
the
early
1980s,
with
the
singer
charting
&quot;I'm
Not
Ready
Yet&quot;,
&quot;Same
Ole
Me
(backed
by
the
<a
href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_Boys?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Oak
Ridge
Boys"
class="mw-redirect">Oak
Ridge
Boys</a>)&quot;,
&quot;Still
Doin'
Time&quot;,
&quot;Tennessee
Whiskey&quot;,
&quot;We
Didn't
See
A
Thing&quot;
(a
duet
with
Ray
Charles)
and
&quot;I
Always
Get
Lucky
With
You&quot;,
which
was
Jones'
last
number
one
in
1984.</p>








<p>In
1981,
Jones
met
Nancy
Sepulvado,
a
34-year-old
divorc&eacute;e
from
Mansfield,
Louisiana.
Sepulvado's
positive
impact
on
Jones'
life
and
career
cannot
be
overestimated;
she
eventually
cleaned
up
his
finances,
kept
him
away
from
his
drug
dealers
(who
reportedly
kidnapped
her
daughter
in
retaliation),
and
managed
his
career.
Jones
always
gave
her
complete
credit
for
saving
his
life.
Nancy,
who
did
not
drink,
explained
to
Nick
Tosches
in
1994,
&quot;He
was
drinking
but
he
was
fun
to
be
around.
It
wasn't
love
at
first
sight
or
anything
like
that.
But
I
saw
what
a
good
person
he
was,
deep
down,
and
I
couldn't
help
caring
about
him.&quot;
Jones
managed
to
quit
cocaine
but
went
on
a
drunken
rampage
in
Alabama
in
the
fall
of
1983
and
was
once
again
straightjacketed
and
committed
to
Hillcrest
Psychiatric
Hospital
suffering
from
malnutrition
and
delusions.
But
by
this
time,
physically
and
emotionally
exhausted,
he
really
did
want
to
quit
drinking.
In
March
1984
in
Birmingham,
Alabama
-
at
the
age
of
52
-
Jones
performed
his
first
stone
cold
sober
show
since
the
early
seventies.
&quot;All
my
life
it
seems
like
I've
been
running
from
something,&quot;
he
told
the
United
Press
International
in
June.
&quot;If
I
knew
what
it
was,
maybe
I
could
run
in
the
right
direction.
But
I
always
seem
to
end
up
going
the
other
way.&quot;
Jones
began
making
up
many
of
the
dates
he
had
missed,
playing
them
for
free
to
pay
back
promoters,
and
began
opening
his
concerts
with
&quot;No
Show
Jones&quot;,
a
song
he
had
written
with
Glen
Martin
that
poked
fun
at
himself
and
other
country
singers.
Jones
always
stressed
that
he
was
not
proud
of
the
way
he
treated
loved
ones
and
friends
over
the
years
and
was
ashamed
of
disappointing
his
fans
when
he
missed
shows,
telling
<i>Billboard</i>
in
2006,
&quot;Now
I
know
it
hurt
my
fans
in
a
way
and
I've
always
been
sad
about
that,
it
really
bothered
me
for
a
long
time.&quot;</p>








<p>Mostly
sober
for
the
rest
of
the
1980s,
Jones
consistently
released
albums
with
Sherrill
producing,
including
<i>Shine
On</i>,
<i>Jones
Country</i>,
<i>You've
Still
Got
A
Place
In
My
Heart</i>,
<i>Who's
Gonna
Fill
Their
Shoes</i>,
<i>Wine
Colored
Roses</i>
(an
album
Jones
would
tell
Jolene
Downs
in
2001
was
one
of
his
personal
favorites),
<i>Too
Wild
Too
Long</i>
and
<i>One
Woman
Man</i>.
Jones'
video
for
his
1985
hit
&quot;Who's
Gonna
Fill
Their
Shoes
won
the
CMA
award
for
Video
of
the
Year
(Billy
Sherrill
makes
a
cameo
as
the
bus
driver).</p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Later_years_and_death_.281990.E2.80.932013.29">Later
years
and
death
(1990–2013)</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=George_Jones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7"
title="Edit
section:
Later
years
and
death
(1990–2013)">edit</a>]</span></h3>








<p>In
1990,
Jones
released
his
last
proper
studio
album
on
Epic,
<i>You
Oughta
Be
Here
With
Me</i>.
Although
the
album
featured
several
stirring
performances,
including
the
lead
single
&quot;Hell
Stays
Open
All
Night
Long&quot;
and
the
Roger
Miller-penned
title
song,
the
single
bombed
and
Jones
made
the
switch
to
MCA,
ending
his
relationship
with
Sherrill
and
what
was
now
<a
href="/wiki/Sony_Music?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Sony
Music"
class="mw-redirect">Sony
Music</a>
after
19
years.
His
first
album
with
MCA,
<i>And
Along
Came
Jones</i>,
was
released
in
1991
and,
backed
by
MCA's
powerful
promotion
team
and
producer
Kyle
Lehning
(who
had
produced
a
string
of
hit
albums
for
<a
href="/wiki/Randy_Travis?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Randy
Travis">Randy
Travis</a>),
the
album
sold
better
than
his
previous
one
had.
However,
two
singles,
&quot;You
Couldn't
Get
The
Picture&quot;
and
&quot;She
Loved
A
Lot
In
Her
Time&quot;
(a
tribute
to
Jones'
mother
Clara),
did
not
crack
the
top
30
on
the
charts
as
Jones
lost
favor
with
country
radio
as
the
format
was
altered
radically
during
the
early
1990s.
His
last
album
to
have
significant
radio
airplay
was
1992's
<i><a
href="/wiki/Walls_Can_Fall?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Walls
Can
Fall">Walls
Can
Fall</a></i>,
which
featured
the
novelty
song
&quot;Finally
Friday&quot;
and
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/I_Don%27t_Need_Your_Rockin%27_Chair?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="I
Don't
Need
Your
Rockin'
Chair">I
Don't
Need
Your
Rockin'
Chair</a>,&quot;
a
testament
to
his
continued
vivaciousness
in
old
age.
Despite
the
loss
of
radio
airplay,
Jones
continued
to
record
and
tour
throughout
the
1990s
and
was
inducted
into
the
Country
Music
Hall
of
Fame
by
Randy
Travis
in
1992.
In
1996,
Jones
released
his
autobiography
<i>I
Lived
To
Tell
It
All</i>
with
Tom
Carter
and
the
irony
of
his
long
career
was
not
lost
on
him,
with
the
singer
writing
in
its
preface,
&quot;I
also
know
that
a
lot
of
my
show-business
peers
are
going
to
be
angry
after
reading
this
book.
So
many
have
worked
so
hard
to
maintain
their
careers.
I
never
took
my
career
seriously,
and
yet
it's
flourishing.&quot;
He
also
pulled
no
punches
about
his
disappointment
in
the
direction
country
music
had
taken,
devoting
a
full
chapter
to
the
changes
in
the
country
music
scene
of
the
1990s
that
saw
him
removed
from
radio
playlists
in
favor
of
a
younger
generation
of
pop-influenced
country
stars.
Despite
his
absence
from
the
country
charts
during
this
time,
latter-day
country
superstars
such
as
<a
href="/wiki/Garth_Brooks?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Garth
Brooks">Garth
Brooks</a>,
Randy
Travis,
<a
href="/wiki/Alan_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Alan
Jackson">Alan
Jackson</a>,
and
many
others
often
paid
tribute
to
Jones
while
expressing
their
love
and
respect
for
his
legacy
as
a
true
country
legend
who
paved
the
way
for
their
own
success.
On
February
17,
1998,
<a
href="/wiki/The_Nashville_Network?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Nashville
Network">The
Nashville
Network</a>
premiered
a
group
of
television
specials
called
<i>The
George
Jones
Show</i>,
with
Jones
as
host.<sup
id="cite_ref-CMTNews_5-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-CMTNews-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>
The
program
featured
informal
chats
with
Jones
holding
court
with
country's
biggest
stars
old
and
new
and,
of
course,
music.
Guests
included
<a
href="/wiki/Loretta_Lynn?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Loretta
Lynn">Loretta
Lynn</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Trace_Adkins?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Trace
Adkins">Trace
Adkins</a>,
Johnny
Paycheck,
Lorrie
Morgan,
Merle
Haggard,
<a
href="/wiki/Billy_Ray_Cyrus?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Billy
Ray
Cyrus">Billy
Ray
Cyrus</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Tim_McGraw?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Tim
McGraw">Tim
McGraw</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Faith_Hill?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Faith
Hill">Faith
Hill</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Charley_Pride?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Charley
Pride">Charley
Pride</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Bobby_Bare?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Bobby
Bare">Bobby
Bare</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Patty_Loveless?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Patty
Loveless">Patty
Loveless</a>
and
Waylon
Jennings,
among
others.</p>








<p>While
Jones
remained
committed
to
&quot;pure
country&quot;,
he
worked
with
the
top
producers
and
musicians
of
the
day
and
the
quality
of
his
work
remained
high.
Some
significant
performances
include
&quot;I
Must
Have
Done
Something
Bad&quot;,
&quot;Wild
Irish
Rose&quot;,
&quot;Billy
B.
Bad&quot;
(a
sarcastic
jab
at
country
music
establishment
trendsetters),
&quot;A
Thousand
Times
A
Day&quot;,
&quot;When
The
Last
Curtain
Falls&quot;
and
the
novelty
&quot;High-Tech
Redneck&quot;.
Jones
most
popular
song
in
his
later
years
was
&quot;Choices&quot;,
the
first
single
from
his
1999
studio
album
<i>Cold
Hard
Truth</i>.
A
video
was
also
made
for
the
song
and
Jones
won
another
Grammy
for
Best
Male
Country
Vocal
Performance.
The
song
was
at
the
center
of
controversy
when
the
Country
Music
Association
invited
Jones
to
perform
it
on
the
awards
show,
but
required
that
he
perform
an
abridged
version.
Jones
refused
and
did
not
attend
the
show.
Alan
Jackson
was
disappointed
with
the
association's
decision
and
halfway
through
his
own
performance
during
the
show
he
signaled
to
his
band
and
played
part
of
Jones'
song
in
protest.</p>








<p>On
March
6,
1999,
Jones
was
involved
in
an
accident
when
he
crashed
his
<a
href="/wiki/Sport_utility_vehicle?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Sport
utility
vehicle">sport
utility
vehicle</a>
near
his
home.
He
was
rushed
to
the
<a
href="/wiki/Vanderbilt_University_Medical_Center?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Vanderbilt
University
Medical
Center">Vanderbilt
University
Medical
Center</a>,
where
he
was
released
two
weeks
later.<sup
id="cite_ref-12"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup>
In
May
of
that
year,
Jones
pleaded
guilty
to
drunk
driving
charges
related
to
the
accident.<sup
id="cite_ref-TAC_13-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-TAC-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup>
The
crash
was
a
significant
turning
point,
as
he
explained
to
<i>Billboard</i>
in
2006:
&quot;...when
I
had
that
wreck
I
made
up
my
mind,
it
put
the
fear
of
God
in
me.
No
more
smoking,
no
more
drinking.
I
didn't
have
to
have
no
help,
I
made
up
my
mind
to
quit.
I
don't
crave
it.&quot;
After
the
accident,
Jones
went
on
to
release
<i>The
Gospel
Collection</i>
in
2003,
which
Billy
Sherrill
came
out
of
retirement
to
produce.<sup
id="cite_ref-TAC_13-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-TAC-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup>
He
appeared
at
a
televised
Johnny
Cash
Memorial
Concert
in
2003,
singing
&quot;Big
River&quot;
with
Willie
Nelson
and
Kris
Kristofferson.
In
2008,
Jones
received
the
Kennedy
Center
Honor
along
with
<a
href="/wiki/Pete_Townshend?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Pete
Townshend">Pete
Townshend</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Roger_Daltrey?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Roger
Daltrey">Roger
Daltrey</a>
of
<a
href="/wiki/The_Who?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Who">The
Who</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Barbra_Streisand?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Barbra
Streisand">Barbra
Streisand</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Morgan_Freeman?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Morgan
Freeman">Morgan
Freeman</a>
and
Twyla
Tharp.
President
<a
href="/wiki/George_W._Bush?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="George
W.
Bush">George
W.
Bush</a>
disclosed
that
he
had
many
of
Jones'
songs
on
his
iPod.
Jones
also
served
as
judge
in
2008
for
the
8th
annual
<a
href="/wiki/The_Independent_Music_Awards?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Independent
Music
Awards"
class="mw-redirect">Independent
Music
Awards</a>
to
support
independent
artists'
careers.<sup
id="cite_ref-14"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup>
and
<i>Rolling
Stone</i>
named
him
number
43
in
their
100
Greatest
Singers
of
All
Time
issue.
An
album
titled
<i>Hits
I
Missed
And
One
I
Didn't</i>,
in
which
he
covered
hits
he
had
passed
on
as
well
as
a
remake
of
his
own
&quot;He
Stopped
Loving
Her
Today&quot;,
would
be
released
as
his
final
studio
album.<sup
id="cite_ref-15"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup>
In
2012,
Jones
received
the
Grammy
Lifetime
Achievement
award.<sup
id="cite_ref-16"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>








<p>On
March
29,
2012,
Jones
was
hospitalized
with
an
upper
respiratory
infection.<sup
id="cite_ref-17"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup>
Months
later,
on
May
21,
Jones
was
hospitalized
again
for
his
infection<sup
id="cite_ref-18"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup>
and
was
released
five
days
later.<sup
id="cite_ref-19"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup>
On
August
14,
2012,
Jones
announced
his
farewell
tour,
the
Grand
Tour,
with
scheduled
stops
at
60
cities.<sup
id="cite_ref-20"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup>
His
final
concert
was
held
in
<a
href="/wiki/Knoxville?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Knoxville"
class="mw-redirect">Knoxville</a>
at
the
Knoxville
City
Coliseum
on
April
6,
2013.</p>








<p>Jones
was
scheduled
to
perform
his
final
concert
at
the
<a
href="/wiki/Bridgestone_Arena?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Bridgestone
Arena">Bridgestone
Arena</a>
on
November
22,
2013.<sup
id="cite_ref-21"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup>
However,
on
April
18,
2013,
Jones
was
admitted
to
Vanderbilt
University
Medical
Center
for
a
slight
fever
and
irregular
blood
pressure.
His
concerts
in
Alabama
and
Salem
were
postponed
as
a
result.<sup
id="cite_ref-22"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup>
While
there,
Jones
died
in
the
early
morning
hours
of
April
26,
2013,
aged
81,
from
hypoxic
respiratory
failure.<sup
id="cite_ref-23"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-24"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup>
Former
first
lady
<a
href="/wiki/Laura_Bush?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Laura
Bush">Laura
Bush</a>
was
among
those
eulogizing
Jones
at
his
funeral
on
May
2,
2013.
Other
speakers
were
Tennessee
governor
<a
href="/wiki/Bill_Haslam?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Bill
Haslam">Bill
Haslam</a>,
news
personality
<a
href="/wiki/Bob_Schieffer?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Bob
Schieffer">Bob
Schieffer</a>,
and
country
singers
Barbara
Mandrell
and
<a
href="/wiki/Kenny_Chesney?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Kenny
Chesney">Kenny
Chesney</a>.
<a
href="/wiki/Alan_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Alan
Jackson">Alan
Jackson</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Kid_Rock?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Kid
Rock">Kid
Rock</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Ronnie_Milsap?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Ronnie
Milsap">Ronnie
Milsap</a>,
Randy
Travis,
Vince
Gill,
Patty
Loveless,
<a
href="/wiki/Travis_Tritt?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Travis
Tritt">Travis
Tritt</a>,
the
Oak
Ridge
Boys,
<a
href="/wiki/Charlie_Daniels?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Charlie
Daniels">Charlie
Daniels</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Wynonna?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Wynonna"
class="mw-redirect">Wynonna</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Brad_Paisley?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Brad
Paisley">Brad
Paisley</a>
provided
musical
tributes<sup
id="cite_ref-25"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup>
The
service
was
broadcast
live
on
CMT,
GAC,
RFD-TV,
<a
href="/wiki/The_Nashville_Network?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="The
Nashville
Network">The
Nashville
Network</a>
and
Family
Net
as
well
as
Nashville
stations.
SiriusXM
and
WSM
650AM,
home
of
the
Grand
Ole
Opry,
broadcast
the
event
on
the
radio.
The
family
has
asked
that
contributions
be
made
to
the
<a
href="/wiki/Grand_Ole_Opry?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Grand
Ole
Opry">Grand
Ole
Opry</a>
Trust
Fund
or
to
the
<a
href="/wiki/Country_Music_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Country
Music
Hall
of
Fame
and
Museum">Country
Music
Hall
of
Fame
and
Museum</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-26"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup>
Jones
death
made
headlines
all
over
the
world;
many
country
stations
abandoned
their
playlists
and
played
his
songs
throughout
the
day.
The
week
after
Jones's
death,
&quot;He
Stopped
Loving
Her
Today&quot;
re-entered
the
hot
country
songs
at
number
21.</p>








<h2><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Legacy">Legacy</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=George_Jones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8"
title="Edit
section:
Legacy">edit</a>]</span></h2>








<p>Part
of
the
reason
Jones
was
such
an
emotive
interpreter
of
country
music
may
have
been
because
he
simply
loved
the
music
more
than
anyone
else;
he
tirelessly
defended
its
integrity.
&quot;It's
never
been
for
the
love
of
money,&quot;
he
told
<i>Billboard</i>
in
2006.
&quot;I
thank
God
for
it
because
it
makes
me
a
living.
But
I
sing
because
I
love
it,
not
because
of
the
dollar
signs.&quot;
Jones
also
went
out
of
his
way
to
promote
younger
country
singers
that
he
felt
were
as
passionate
about
the
music
as
he
was.
&quot;Everybody
knows
he's
a
great
singer,&quot;
Alan
Jackson
stated
in
1995,
&quot;but
what
I
like
most
about
George
is
that
when
you
meet
him,
he
is
like
some
ole
guy
that
works
down
at
the
gas
station...even
though
he's
a
legend!&quot;
Jones
influence
as
a
singer
is
beyond
question.
Shortly
after
his
death,
Andrew
Mueller
wrote
in
<i><a
href="/wiki/Uncut_(magazine)?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Uncut
(magazine)">Uncut</a></i>,
&quot;He
was
one
of
the
finest
interpretive
singers
who
ever
lifted
a
microphone...There
cannot
be
a
single
country
songwriter
of
the
last
50-odd
years
who
has
not
wondered
what
it
might
be
like
to
hear
their
words
sung
by
that
voice.&quot;
In
1994,
country
music
historian
Colin
Escott
pronounced,
&quot;Contemporary
country
music
is
virtually
founded
on
reverence
for
George
Jones.
Walk
through
a
room
of
country
singers
and
conduct
a
quick
poll,
George
nearly
always
tops
it.&quot;
In
the
liner
notes
to
<i>Essential
George
Jones:
The
Spirit
of
Country</i>
Richard
Kienzle
confirms,
&quot;Jones
sings
of
people
and
stories
that
are
achingly
human.
He
can
turn
a
ballad
into
a
catharsis
by
wringing
every
possible
emotion
from
it,
making
it
a
primal,
strangled
cry
of
anguish&quot;.
In
a
quote
that
appeared
on
the
sleeve
of
Jones'
1976
album
<i>Memories
Of
Us</i>,
<a
href="/wiki/Emmylou_Harris?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Emmylou
Harris">Emmylou
Harris</a>
wrote,
&quot;...when
you
hear
George
Jones
sing,
you
are
hearing
a
man
who
takes
a
song
and
makes
it
a
work
of
art
-
always.&quot;
In
<i>Same
Ole
Me</i>,
several
country
music
stars
offer
similar
thoughts:
Johnny
Cash:
&quot;When
people
ask
me
who
my
favorite
country
singer
is,
I
say,
'You
mean
besides
George
Jones?'&quot;;
Randy
Travis:
&quot;It
sounds
like
he's
lived
every
minute
of
every
word
that
he
sings
and
there's
very
few
people
who
can
do
that&quot;;
<a
href="/wiki/Tom_T._Hall?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Tom
T.
Hall">Tom
T.
Hall</a>:
&quot;It
was
always
Jones
who
got
the
message
across
just
right&quot;;
and
Roy
Acuff:
&quot;I'd
give
anything
if
I
could
sing
like
George
Jones&quot;.
In
the
same
documentary,
producer
Billy
Sherrill
states,
&quot;All
I
did
was
change
the
instrumentation
around
him.
I
don't
think
he's
changed
<i>at
all</i>.&quot;
Jones
dedication
to
what
he
called
&quot;hardcore
country
music&quot;
remained
resolute
until
the
day
he
died.</p>








<h2><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Influence_Beyond_Country_Music">Influence
Beyond
Country
Music</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=George_Jones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9"
title="Edit
section:
Influence
Beyond
Country
Music">edit</a>]</span></h2>








<p>Unlike
some
of
his
contemporaries,
Jones
stuck
with
country
music
with
a
vengeance.
Ironically,
without
even
trying,
Jones'
unabashed
loyalty
to
strictly
country
arrangements
attracted
the
admiration
of
musicians
and
songwriters
from
a
wide
range
of
genres.
In
an
often-quoted
tribute,
Frank
Sinatra
called
Jones
&quot;the
second
best
singer
in
America&quot;.
In
a
<i>Rolling
Stone</i>
interview
in
1969,
<a
href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Bob
Dylan">Bob
Dylan</a>
was
asked
what
he
thought
was
the
best
song
released
in
the
previous
year
and
he
replied,
&quot;George
Jones
had
one
called
'Small
Town
Laboring
Man'.&quot;
In
his
autobiography
<i>Chronicles</i>,
Dylan
states
that
in
the
early
1960s
he
was
largely
unimpressed
by
what
he
heard
on
the
radio
and
admits
&quot;Outside
of
maybe
George
Jones,
I
didn't
listen
to
country
music
either.&quot;
Country
rock
pioneer
<a
href="/wiki/Gram_Parsons?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Gram
Parsons">Gram
Parsons</a>
was
an
avid
George
Jones
fan
and
covered
Jones'
song
&quot;That's
All
It
Took&quot;
on
his
first
solo
album.
In
the
documentary
<i>Gram
Parsons:
Fallen
Angel</i>,
Pamela
des
Barres
recalls
seeing
Parsons
singing
Jones'
song
&quot;She
Once
Lived
Here&quot;
at
an
empty
Whiskey
A
Go
Go
in
Los
Angeles:
&quot;It
was
my
peak,
<i>peak</i>
moment,
not
sitting
on
Jimmy
Page's
amp...that
was
my
peak
moment.&quot;
Parsons
reignited
Keith
Richards'
interest
in
country
music
in
the
early
seventies
and
after
Jones'
death
in
2013
the
guitarist
wrote,
&quot;He
possessed
the
most
touching
voice,
the
most
expressive
ways
of
projecting
that
beautiful
instrument
of
anyone
I
can
call
to
mind.
You
heard
his
heart
in
every
note
he
sang.&quot;
Richards
recorded
&quot;Say
It's
Not
You&quot;
with
Jones
for
<i>The
Bradley
Barn
Sessions</i>
in
1994
and
recalls
in
his
autobiography
hearing
him
sing
for
the
first
time
when
the
<a
href="/wiki/Rolling_Stones?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Rolling
Stones"
class="mw-redirect">Rolling
Stones</a>
and
Jones
were
on
the
same
show
in
Texas
in
1964:
&quot;They
trailed
in
with
tumbleweed
following
them,
as
if
tumbleweed
was
their
pet.
Dust
all
over
the
place,
a
bunch
of
cowboys.
But
when
George
got
up,
we
went
whoa,
there's
a
master
up
there.&quot;
In
an
interview
for
the
1989
Rolling
Stones
documentary
<i>25x5</i>,
<a
href="/wiki/Mick_Jagger?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Mick
Jagger">Mick
Jagger</a>
also
cites
Jones
as
one
of
his
favorite
country
singers.
<a
href="/wiki/John_Prine?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="John
Prine">John
Prine</a>
mentions
Jones
in
his
song
&quot;Jesus
the
Missing
Years&quot;.
Jones
fan
Elvis
Costello
had
a
surprise
hit
in
the
UK
when
he
covered
&quot;A
Good
Year
For
The
Roses&quot;
in
1981.
<a
href="/wiki/Elliott_Smith?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Elliott
Smith">Elliott
Smith</a>
told
an
interviewer
about
his
idea
of
Heaven:
&quot;George
Jones
would
be
singing
all
the
time.
It
would
be
like
New
York
in
reverse:
people
would
be
nice
to
each
other
for
no
reason
at
all,
and
it
would
smell
good.&quot;
In
a
2001
interview
with
Mark
Binelli
from
<i>Rolling
Stone</i>,
<a
href="/wiki/Leonard_Cohen?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Leonard
Cohen">Leonard
Cohen</a>
asked,
&quot;Have
you
heard
George
Jones'
last
record
<i>Cold
Hard
Truth</i>?
I
love
to
hear
an
old
guy
lay
out
his
situation.
He
has
the
best
voice
in
America.&quot;
The
day
Jones
died,
Cohen
performed
&quot;Choices&quot;
on
stage
in
Winnipeg,
Canada
as
a
tribute
to
the
country
legend.
In
2013,
<a
href="/wiki/Robbie_Robertson?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Robbie
Robertson">Robbie
Robertson</a>
told
<i>Uncut</i>,
&quot;He
was
the
Ray
Charles
of
country
music
-
the
one
who
could
make
you
cry
with
his
voice...We
wouldn't
listen
to
country
music,
the
guys
in
the
Band,
but
we'd
listen
to
George
Jones...&quot;
James
Taylor,
who
wrote
&quot;Bartenders
Blues&quot;
with
Jones
in
mind
and
sang
background
vocals
with
him
on
the
recording,
told
<i>Rolling
Stone</i>,
&quot;He
sounds
like
a
steel
guitar.
It's
the
way
he
blends
notes,
the
way
he
comes
up
to
them,
the
way
he
crescendos
and
decrescendos.
The
dynamic
of
it
is
very
tight
and
very
controlled
-
it's
like
carving
with
the
voice.&quot;
Other
disparate
artists
who
recorded
with
Jones
include
Dennis
Locorriere
and
Ray
Sawyer
of
<a
href="/wiki/Dr._Hook?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Dr.
Hook"
class="mw-redirect">Dr.
Hook</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Mark_Knopfler?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Mark
Knopfler">Mark
Knopfler</a>,
the
<a
href="/wiki/Staples_Singers?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Staples
Singers"
class="mw-redirect">Staples
Singers</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Leon_Russell?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Leon
Russell">Leon
Russell</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/B.B_King?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="B.B
King"
class="mw-redirect">B.B
King</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Linda_Ronstadt?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Linda
Ronstadt">Linda
Ronstadt</a>.
In
1995,
<a
href="/wiki/Burt_Reynolds?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Burt
Reynolds">Burt
Reynolds</a>
wrote,
&quot;He
is
to
country
music
what
<a
href="/wiki/Spencer_Tracy?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Spencer
Tracy">Spencer
Tracy</a>
is
to
movies.&quot;
On
the
day
of
his
death,
<a
href="/wiki/Nathan_Followill?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Nathan
Followill"
class="mw-redirect">Nathan
Followill</a>
of
the
alternative
rock
band
<a
href="/wiki/Kings_of_Leon?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Kings
of
Leon">Kings
of
Leon</a>
tweeted,
&quot;RIP,
George
Jones,
you'll
be
sorely
missed.
One
of
the
all
time
greats.&quot;</p>








<h2><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Duets">Duets</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=George_Jones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10"
title="Edit
section:
Duets">edit</a>]</span></h2>








<p>Jones
was
one
of
the
greatest
harmony
singers
in
country
music
and
released
many
duets
over
the
course
of
his
long
career.
While
his
songs
with
Tammy
Wynette
are
his
most
celebrated,
Jones
claimed
in
his
autobiography
that
he
felt
his
duets
with
Melba
Montgomery
were
his
best.
Jones
also
recorded
duet
albums
with
<a
href="/wiki/Gene_Pitney?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"
title="Gene
Pitney">Gene
Pitney</a>
and
his
former
bass
player
Johnny
Paycheck.
George's
record
with
Paycheck,
1980's
<i>Double
Trouble</i>,
is
one
of
his
most
atypical
records
and
features
him
giving
credible
performances
on
numbers
like
&quot;Maybelline&quot;,
&quot;You
Better
Move
On&quot;,
and
&quot;Proud
Mary&quot;.
Jones
also
recorded
the
duet
albums
<i>My
Very
Special
Guests</i>
(1979),
<i>Ladies
Choice</i>
(1984),
<i>Friends
In
High
Places</i>
(1991),
<i>The
Bradley
Barn
Sessions</i>
(1994),
<i>God's
Country:
George
Jones
And
Friends</i>
(2006),
a
second
album
with
Merle
Haggard
called
<i>Kickin'
Out
The
Footlights...Again</i>
(2006)
and
<i>Burn
Your
Playhouse
Down</i>
(2008).</p>








<p>Some
notable
duets
Jones
recorded
include:</p>

class="div-col
columns

35em;
-webkit-column-width:
35em;
column-width:

Sure
Make
Good
Love&quot;
(with
Loretta
Lynn)</li>

Be
Ashamed
Of
Your
Age&quot;
(with
Jerry
Lee
Lewis)</li>

Don't

Never
Comes&quot;

Lawrence)</li>

In

(with

So&quot;

Country
Boys&quot;

I
Come&quot;

Tubb</a>)</li>

&amp;

Say
It's

Cash)</li>

This

With
Me&quot;

Mark
Chestnutt)</li>

World
Outside&quot;
&amp;
&quot;The
Window
Up
Above&quot;
(with
<a
href="/wiki/Ralph_Stanley?qsrc=3044"
onmousedown="triggerActionLog('primaryContent','','center','click','primaryContent','','');"

<h2><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Personal_life">Personal
life</span>
<span
class="mw-editsection">[<a
href="/wiki?title=George_Jones&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12"
title="Edit
section:
Personal
life">edit</a>]</span></h2>








<p>Jones
married
his
first
wife,
Dorothy
Bonvillion,
in
1950
and
had
one
daughter,
Susan.
In
1989,
Jones
admitted
in
the
<i>Same
Ole
Me</i>
retrospective,
&quot;I
had
my
first
daughter
by
my
first
wife
and,
to
be
honest
with
you,
that's
the
main
reason
I
went
into
the
service,
because
I
couldn't
pay
my
child
support.&quot;
He
had
two
sons,
Jeffery
and
Brian,
with
his
second
wife
Shirley;
he
suffered
a
strained
relationship
with
them,
no
doubt
in
part
because
of
his
extended
absences,
drinking
and
erratic
personal
behavior.
In
2008,
Jones
recorded
&quot;You
And
Me
And
Time&quot;
with
his
daughter
by
Tammy,
Tamala
Georgette.
In
2011,
Georgette
published
a
book,
<i>The
Three
Of
Us:
Understanding
My
Mother,
Finding
My
Father,
and
Growing
Up
with
George
and
Tammy</i>.
Jones
was
reportedly
unhappy
about
the
book.</p>

<script
type="text/javascript">




render_ads(false,
window['ad_config_bot']);





function
afc_match()
{








var
count
=
-1;








if(window['ad_config_top'])
{












count
=
window['ad_config_top'];








}









var
countBottom
=
0;








if(window['ad_config_bot'])
{












countBottom
=
window['ad_config_bot'];








}









if(window['backfillParam'])
{




























window['backfillParam']['pl_bmodn']
=
5;
























window['backfillParam']['pl_tnum']
=
count;












window['backfillParam']['pl_bnum']
=
countBottom;








}


































var
_matchUrl
=
"/afc-match?q=George_Jones&page=1&ac=24&qid=20412EA76C1D7AC9B183301DE53167B4&qsrc=121&dm=&qrt=2&lid="+
"&o=0&l=dir&oo=&cl=ca-aj-cat&ch=cx_ask_wiki,ad_dirN";





















_matchUrl+=
"&userip=76.78.3.114";




































_matchUrl+="&losid=a&locid=encw&lodid=us&loaid=a16";









var
location
=
window.location
+
"";








location
=
location.substring(7);








var
cutIdx
=
location.indexOf("/");








if
(location.indexOf(":")
>
-1)
{




















cutIdx
=
1
+
cutIdx
+
location.substring(cutIdx+1).indexOf("/");








}









var
urlPrefix
=
location.substring(0,
cutIdx);








_matchUrl
=
"http://"
+
urlPrefix
+
_matchUrl
+
"&afcec="
+
count
+
"&afcecb="
+
countBottom;








ajax_get(_matchUrl,
null,
null);




}





function
getXHR()
{








if
(window.XMLHttpRequest)
{












return
new
XMLHttpRequest();








}
else
if
(window.ActiveXObject)
{












try
{
















return
new
ActiveXObject("Msxml2.XMLHTTP");












}
catch
(e)
{
















try
{




















return
new
ActiveXObject("Microsoft.XMLHTTP");
















}
catch
(E)
{




















return
null;
















}












}








}








else
{












return
null;








}




}





function
ajax_get(url,
onsuccess,
onerror)
{








var
obj
=
getXHR();








if(obj)
{












obj.onreadystatechange
=
function()
{
















if(obj.readyState==4)
{




















if
(obj.status
==
200
&&
typeof(onsuccess)
==
"function")
{
























onsuccess(obj);




















}
else
if
(typeof(onerror)
==
"function")
{
























onerror(obj);




















}






















try
{
























delete
obj['onreadystatechange'];




















}
catch(e)
{}
















}












};












obj.open("GET",
url,
true,
"",
"");












obj.send(null);












return
obj;








}
else
{












if(onerror)
{
















onerror("Error
creating
XHR");












}
else
{
















a10.debug("Error
creating
XHR");












}












return
null;








}




}





afc_match();

</script>

function
addEvent(obj,
evType,
fn){












if
(obj.addEventListener){
















obj.addEventListener(evType,
fn,
false);

true;












}

=

fn);

}

{
















return
false;












}








}









function
